<p><b>Insects</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">insectum</i></span></i>) are <a href="/wiki/Pancrustacea" title="Pancrustacea">pancrustacean</a> <a href="/wiki/Hexapoda" title="Hexapoda">hexapod</a> <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">class</a> <b>Insecta</b>. They are the largest group within the <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropod</a> <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phylum</a>. Insects have a <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitinous</a> <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a>, a three-part body (<a href="/wiki/Head" title="Head">head</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorax_(insect_anatomy)" title="Thorax (insect anatomy)">thorax</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdomen_(insect_anatomy)" title="Abdomen (insect anatomy)">abdomen</a>), three pairs of jointed <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg" title="Arthropod leg">legs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compound_eye" title="Compound eye">compound eyes</a> and one pair of <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a>. Their blood is not totally contained in vessels; some circulates in an open cavity known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haemocoel" title="Haemocoel">haemocoel</a>. Insects are the most diverse group of animals; they include more than a million described <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> and represent more than half of all known living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The total number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> species is estimated at between six and ten million;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-number_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-number-4">[4]</a></sup> potentially over 90% of the animal life forms on Earth are insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-number_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-number-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Insects may be found in nearly all <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environments</a>, although only a small number of species reside in the oceans, which are dominated by another arthropod group, <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, which recent research has indicated insects are nested within.
</p><p>Nearly all insects hatch from <a href="/wiki/Egg" title="Egg">eggs</a>. Insect growth is constrained by the inelastic <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a> and development involves a series of <a href="/wiki/Moulting" title="Moulting">molts</a>. The immature stages often differ from the adults in structure, habit and habitat, and can include a usually immobile <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupal</a> stage in those groups that undergo  <a href="/wiki/Holometabolism" title="Holometabolism">four-stage metamorphosis</a>. Insects that undergo <a href="/wiki/Hemimetabolism" title="Hemimetabolism">three-stage metamorphosis</a> lack a pupal stage and adults develop through a series of <a href="/wiki/Nymph_(biology)" title="Nymph (biology)">nymphal</a> stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgraw-hill_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcgraw-hill-6">[6]</a></sup> The higher level relationship of the <a href="/wiki/Hexapoda" title="Hexapoda">insects</a> is unclear. Fossilized insects of enormous size have been found from the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> Era, including <a href="/wiki/Meganisoptera" title="Meganisoptera">giant dragonflies</a> with wingspans of 55 to 70 cm (22 to 28 in). The most diverse insect groups appear to have <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">coevolved</a> with <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>.
</p><p>Adult insects typically move about by walking, flying, or sometimes swimming. As it allows for rapid yet stable movement, many insects adopt a tripedal gait in which they walk with their legs touching the ground in alternating triangles, composed of the front and rear on one side with the middle on the other side. Insects are the only invertebrate group with members able to achieve sustained powered flight, and all flying insects derive from one common ancestor. Many insects spend at least part of their lives under water, with <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larval</a> adaptations that include <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a>, and some adult insects are aquatic and have adaptations for swimming. Some species, such as <a href="/wiki/Gerridae" title="Gerridae">water striders</a>, are capable of walking on the surface of water. Insects are mostly solitary, but some, such as certain <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>, are social and live in large, well-organized colonies. Some insects, such as <a href="/wiki/Earwig" title="Earwig">earwigs</a>, show maternal care, guarding their eggs and young. Insects can communicate with each other in a variety of ways. Male <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moths</a> can sense the <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a> of female moths over great distances. Other species communicate with sounds: <a href="/wiki/Cricket_(insect)" title="Cricket (insect)">crickets</a> <a href="/wiki/Stridulation" title="Stridulation">stridulate</a>, or rub their wings together, to attract a mate and repel other males. <a href="/wiki/Firefly" title="Firefly">Lampyrid</a> <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a> communicate with light.
</p><p>Humans regard certain insects as <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">pests</a>, and attempt to control them using <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a>, and a host of other techniques. Some insects damage crops by feeding on sap, leaves, fruits, or wood. Some species are <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasitic</a>, and may <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vector</a> diseases. Some insects perform complex ecological roles; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blow-flies" title="Blow-flies">blow-flies</a>, for example, help consume <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a> but also spread diseases. Insect <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollinators</a> are essential to the life cycle of many flowering plant species on which most organisms, including humans, are at least partly dependent; without them, the terrestrial portion of the biosphere would be devastated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Many insects are considered ecologically beneficial as predators and a few provide direct economic benefit. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silkworm" title="Silkworm">Silkworms</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> and honey bees produce <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> and both have been domesticated by humans. Insects are consumed as food in 80% of the world's nations, by people in roughly 3000 ethnic groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theguardian_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramos-Elorduy_1998_44_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramos-Elorduy_1998_44-9">[9]</a></sup> Human activities also have effects on <a href="/wiki/Insect_biodiversity" title="Insect biodiversity">insect biodiversity</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word <i>insect</i> comes from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> word <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/insectum" title="wikt:insectum">insectum</a></i></span>, meaning "with a notched or divided body", or literally "cut into", from the neuter singular perfect passive participle of <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/insectare" title="wikt:insectare">insectare</a></i></span>, "to cut into, to cut up", from <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">in-</i></span> "into" and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">secare</i></span> "to cut";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline-10">[10]</a></sup> because insects appear "cut into" three sections. A <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">ἔντομον</span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">éntomon</i></span>), "cut into sections", <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> introduced the Latin designation as a loan-translation of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%94%CE%BD%CF%84%CE%BF%CE%BC%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:ἔντομος">ἔντομος</a></span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">éntomos</i></span>) or "insect" (as in <a href="/wiki/Entomology" title="Entomology">entomology</a>), which was <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s term for this class of life, also in reference to their "notched" bodies. The word <i>insect</i> first appears documented in English in 1601 in <a href="/wiki/Philemon_Holland" title="Philemon Holland">Holland</a>'s translation of Pliny. Translations of Aristotle's term also form the usual word for <i>insect</i> in <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> (<span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/trychfil" title="wikt:trychfil">trychfil</a></i></span>, from <span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/trychu" title="wikt:trychu">trychu</a></i></span> "to cut" and <span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">mil</i></span>, "animal"), <a href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian" title="Serbo-Croatian">Serbo-Croatian</a> (<span title="Serbo-Croatian-language text"><i lang="sh">zareznik</i></span>, from <span title="Serbo-Croatian-language text"><i lang="sh">rezati</i></span>, "to cut"), <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D0%BD%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B5" title="wikt:насекомое">насекомое</a></span></span> [<span title="Russian-language romanization"><i lang="ru-Latn">nasekomoje</i></span>], from <span title="Russian-language romanization"><i lang="ru-Latn">seč'/-sekat</i></span>, "to cut"), etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>In common parlance, insects are also called <b>bugs</b>, though this term usually includes all terrestrial arthropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[a]</a></sup>  The term is also occasionally extended to colloquial names for freshwater or marine crustaceans (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balmain_bug" title="Balmain bug">Balmain bug</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moreton_Bay_bug" title="Moreton Bay bug">Moreton Bay bug</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mudbug" title="Mudbug">mudbug</a>) and used by physicians and bacteriologists for disease-causing germs (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superbug" title="Superbug">superbugs</a>), but entomologists to some extent reserve this term for a narrow category of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/True_bugs" title="True bugs">true bugs</a>", insects of the order <a href="/wiki/Hemiptera" title="Hemiptera">Hemiptera</a>, such as cicadas and shield bugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walbauer1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walbauer1-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span></h2>
<p>The precise definition of the taxon Insecta and the equivalent English name "insect" varies; three alternative definitions are shown in the table.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Definition of Insecta
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Group</th>
<th colspan="3">Alternative definitions
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collembola" title="Collembola">Collembola</a> (springtails)</td>
<td rowspan="6">Insecta <i>sensu lato</i><br/>= Hexapoda</td>
<td rowspan="3">"<a href="/wiki/Entognatha" title="Entognatha">Entognatha</a>"</td>
<td rowspan="5">"<a href="/wiki/Apterygota" title="Apterygota">Apterygota</a>"<br/>(wingless hexapods)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Protura" title="Protura">Protura</a> (coneheads)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Diplura" title="Diplura">Diplura</a> (two-pronged bristletails)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Archaeognatha" title="Archaeognatha">Archaeognatha</a> (jumping bristletails)</td>
<td rowspan="3">Insecta <i>sensu stricto</i><br/>= Ectognatha
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Zygentoma" title="Zygentoma">Zygentoma</a> (silverfish)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Pterygota" title="Pterygota">Pterygota</a> (winged insects)</td>
<td>Insecta <i>sensu strictissimo</i>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the broadest <a href="/wiki/Circumscription_(taxonomy)" title="Circumscription (taxonomy)">circumscription</a>, Insecta <i><a href="/wiki/Sensu" title="Sensu">sensu lato</a></i> consists of all <a href="/wiki/Hexapoda" title="Hexapoda">hexapods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sasaki_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sasaki-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChinery199310_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChinery199310-17">[16]</a></sup> Traditionally, insects defined in this way were divided into "Apterygota" (the first five groups in the table)—the wingless insects—and <a href="/wiki/Pterygota" title="Pterygota">Pterygota</a>—the winged and secondarily wingless insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> However, modern phylogenetic studies have shown that "Apterygota" is not monophyletic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kjer_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kjer-19">[18]</a></sup> and so does not form a good taxon. A narrower circumscription restricts insects to those hexapods with external mouthparts, and comprises only the last three groups in the table. In this sense, Insecta <i><a href="/wiki/Sensu" title="Sensu">sensu stricto</a></i> is equivalent to Ectognatha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sasaki_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sasaki-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kjer_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kjer-19">[18]</a></sup> In the narrowest circumscription, insects are restricted to hexapods that are either winged or descended from winged ancestors. Insecta <i><a href="/wiki/Sensu" title="Sensu">sensu strictissimo</a></i> is then equivalent to Pterygota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HughLong16_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-HughLong16-20">[19]</a></sup> For the purposes of this article, the middle definition is used; insects consist of two wingless taxa, <a href="/wiki/Archaeognatha" title="Archaeognatha">Archaeognatha</a> (jumping bristletails) and <a href="/wiki/Zygentoma" title="Zygentoma">Zygentoma</a> (silverfish), plus the winged or secondarily wingless Pterygota.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny_and_evolution">Phylogeny and evolution</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_insects" title="Evolution of insects">Evolution of insects</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="External_phylogeny">External phylogeny</span></h3>
<p>Although traditionally grouped with <a href="/wiki/Millipede" title="Millipede">millipedes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Centipede" title="Centipede">centipedes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garwood_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garwood-21">[20]</a></sup> more recent analysis closer <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">evolutionary ties</a> with <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Pancrustacea" title="Pancrustacea">Pancrustacea</a> theory, insects, together with <a href="/wiki/Entognatha" title="Entognatha">Entognatha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Remipedia" title="Remipedia">Remipedia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cephalocarida" title="Cephalocarida">Cephalocarida</a>, form a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a>, the Pancrustacea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> Insects form a single clade, closely related to crustaceans and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myriapod" title="Myriapod">myriapods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-misof_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-misof-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other terrestrial arthropods, such as <a href="/wiki/Centipede" title="Centipede">centipedes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millipede" title="Millipede">millipedes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scorpion" title="Scorpion">scorpions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodlouse" title="Woodlouse">woodlice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a> are sometimes confused with insects since their body plans can appear similar, sharing (as do all arthropods) a jointed exoskeleton. However, upon closer examination, their features differ significantly; most noticeably, they do not have the six-legged characteristic of adult insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogenetic" title="Phylogenetic">phylogenetic</a> tree of the arthropods places the insects in the context of other hexapods and the crustaceans, and the more distantly-related myriapods and chelicerates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature_phylo_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature_phylo-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Insect_antennae_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Insect_antennae_comparison.jpg/220px-Insect_antennae_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Insect_antennae_comparison.jpg/330px-Insect_antennae_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Insect_antennae_comparison.jpg/440px-Insect_antennae_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Insect_antennae_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Evolution has produced enormous variety in insects. Pictured are some possible shapes of <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Four large-scale radiations of insects have occurred: <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a> (from about 300 million years ago), <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">flies</a> (from about 250 million years ago), <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a> (both from about 150 million years ago).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiegmann_Trautwein_Winkler_Barr_2011_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wiegmann_Trautwein_Winkler_Barr_2011-26">[25]</a></sup> These four groups account for the majority of described species. 
</p><p>The origins of <a href="/wiki/Insect_flight" title="Insect flight">insect flight</a> remain obscure, since the earliest winged insects currently known appear to have been capable fliers. Some extinct insects had an additional pair of winglets attaching to the first segment of the thorax, for a total of three pairs. As of 2009, no evidence suggests the insects were a particularly successful group of animals before they evolved to have wings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pennsylvanian_(geology)" title="Pennsylvanian (geology)">Late Carboniferous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cisuralian" title="Cisuralian">Early Permian</a> insect orders include both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> groups, their stem groups,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archi_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Archi-28">[27]</a></sup> and a number of <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> groups, now extinct. During this era, some giant dragonfly-like forms reached wingspans of 55 to 70 cm (22 to 28 in), making them far larger than any living insect. This gigantism may have been due to higher <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_oxygen" title="Geological history of oxygen">atmospheric oxygen</a> levels that allowed increased respiratory efficiency relative to today. The lack of flying vertebrates could have been another factor. Most extinct orders of insects developed during the Permian period that began around 270 million years ago. Many of the early groups became extinct during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permian-Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian-Triassic extinction event">Permian-Triassic extinction event</a>, the largest mass extinction in the history of the Earth, around 252 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_of_Insects_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Insects-29">[28]</a></sup> In 2008, researchers at <a href="/wiki/Tufts_University" title="Tufts University">Tufts University</a> uncovered what they believe is the world's oldest known full-body impression of a primitive flying insect, a 300-million-year-old specimen from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carboniferous_period" title="Carboniferous period">Carboniferous period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> The oldest insect fossil was considered to be the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Rhyniognatha" title="Rhyniognatha">Rhyniognatha hirsti</a></i>, from the 396-million-year-old <a href="/wiki/Rhynie_chert" title="Rhynie chert">Rhynie chert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EngelGrim_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-EngelGrim-31">[30]</a></sup> However, other analyses have disputed this placement, finding it to be more likely a myriapod.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The remarkably successful <a href="/wiki/Hymenoptera" title="Hymenoptera">Hymenoptera</a> (wasps, bees, and ants) appeared as long as 200 million years ago in the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> period, but achieved their wide diversity more recently in the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> era, which began 66 million years ago. Some highly successful insect groups evolved in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>, a powerful illustration of coevolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biology-coevolution_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biology-coevolution-33">[32]</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_phylogeny">Internal phylogeny</span></h3>
<p>The internal phylogeny is based on the works of Sroka, Staniczek &amp; Bechly 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> Prokop et al. 2017<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> and Wipfler et al. 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Insect_orders" title="Category:Insect orders">Category:Insect orders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Insect_families" title="Category:Insect families">Category:Insect families</a></div>
<p>Traditional morphology-based or appearance-based <a href="/wiki/Systematics" title="Systematics">systematics</a> have usually given the <a href="/wiki/Hexapoda" title="Hexapoda">Hexapoda</a> the rank of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superclass_(biology)" title="Superclass (biology)">superclass</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 180">: 180 </span></sup> and identified four groups within it: insects (Ectognatha), springtails (<a href="/wiki/Springtail" title="Springtail">Collembola</a>), <a href="/wiki/Protura" title="Protura">Protura</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diplura" title="Diplura">Diplura</a>, the latter three being grouped together as the <a href="/wiki/Entognatha" title="Entognatha">Entognatha</a> on the basis of internalized mouth parts. Supraordinal relationships have undergone numerous changes with the advent of methods based on evolutionary history and genetic data. A recent theory is that the Hexapoda are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyphyletic" title="Polyphyletic">polyphyletic</a> (where the last common ancestor was not a member of the group), with the entognath classes having separate evolutionary histories from the Insecta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> Many of the traditional appearance-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxa" title="Taxa">taxa</a> are paraphyletic, so rather than using ranks like <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">subclass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">superorder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">infraorder</a>, it has proved better to use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> groupings (in which the last common ancestor is a member of the group). The following represents the best-supported monophyletic groupings for the Insecta.
</p><p>Insects can be divided into two groups historically treated as subclasses: wingless insects, known as Apterygota, and winged insects, known as Pterygota. The Apterygota consist of the primitively wingless order of the silverfish (Zygentoma). Archaeognatha make up the Monocondylia based on the shape of their <a href="/wiki/Mandible_(insect_mouthpart)" title="Mandible (insect mouthpart)">mandibles</a>, while Zygentoma and Pterygota are grouped together as Dicondylia. The Zygentoma themselves possibly are not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a>, with the family <a href="/wiki/Lepidotrichidae" title="Lepidotrichidae">Lepidotrichidae</a> being a <a href="/wiki/Sister_group" title="Sister group">sister group</a> to the Dicondylia (Pterygota and the remaining Zygentoma).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Classification_of_Insects_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Classification_of_Insects-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paleoptera and Neoptera are the winged orders of insects differentiated by the presence of hardened body parts called <a href="/wiki/Sclerite" title="Sclerite">sclerites</a>, and in the Neoptera, muscles that allow their wings to fold flatly over the abdomen. Neoptera can further be divided into incomplete metamorphosis-based (<a href="/wiki/Polyneoptera" title="Polyneoptera">Polyneoptera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paraneoptera" title="Paraneoptera">Paraneoptera</a>) and complete metamorphosis-based groups. It has proved difficult to clarify the relationships between the orders in Polyneoptera because of constant new findings calling for revision of the taxa. For example, the Paraneoptera have turned out to be more closely related to the Endopterygota than to the rest of the Exopterygota. The recent molecular finding that the traditional louse orders <a href="/wiki/Mallophaga" title="Mallophaga">Mallophaga</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anoplura" title="Anoplura">Anoplura</a> are derived from within <a href="/wiki/Psocoptera" title="Psocoptera">Psocoptera</a> has led to the new taxon <a href="/wiki/Psocodea" title="Psocodea">Psocodea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Phasmatodea" title="Phasmatodea">Phasmatodea</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embiidina" title="Embiidina">Embiidina</a> have been suggested to form the Eukinolabia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> Mantodea, Blattodea, and Isoptera are thought to form a monophyletic group termed <a href="/wiki/Dictyoptera" title="Dictyoptera">Dictyoptera</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Exopterygota likely are paraphyletic in regard to the Endopterygota. The Neuropterida are often lumped or split on the whims of the taxonomist. Fleas are now thought to be closely related to boreid mecopterans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> Many questions remain in the basal relationships among endopterygote orders, particularly the Hymenoptera.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_relationships">Evolutionary relationships</span></h3>
<p>Insects are prey for a variety of organisms, including terrestrial vertebrates. The earliest vertebrates on land existed 400 million years ago and were large amphibious <a href="/wiki/Piscivore" title="Piscivore">piscivores</a>. Through gradual evolutionary change, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insectivory" title="Insectivory">insectivory</a> was the next diet type to evolve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Insects were among the earliest terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a> and acted as major selection agents on plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biology-coevolution_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Biology-coevolution-33">[32]</a></sup> Plants evolved chemical <a href="/wiki/Plant_defense_against_herbivory" title="Plant defense against herbivory">defenses against this herbivory</a> and the insects, in turn, evolved mechanisms to deal with plant toxins. Many insects make use of these toxins to protect themselves from their predators. Such insects often advertise their toxicity using warning colors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-46">[45]</a></sup> This successful evolutionary pattern has also been used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mimic" title="Mimic">mimics</a>. Over time, this has led to complex groups of coevolved species. Conversely, some interactions between plants and insects, like <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a>, are beneficial to both organisms. Coevolution has led to the development of very specific <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualisms</a> in such systems.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity">Diversity</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eukaryote_species_pie_tree.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="624" data-file-width="1117" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Eukaryote_species_pie_tree.png/220px-Eukaryote_species_pie_tree.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Eukaryote_species_pie_tree.png/330px-Eukaryote_species_pie_tree.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Eukaryote_species_pie_tree.png/440px-Eukaryote_species_pie_tree.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eukaryote_species_pie_tree.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pie chart of described <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryote</a> species, showing just over half of these to be insects</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Insect_biodiversity" title="Insect biodiversity">Insect biodiversity</a></div>
<p>Estimates of the total number of insect species, or those within specific <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">orders</a>, often vary considerably. Globally, averages of these estimates suggest there are around 1.5 million beetle species and 5.5 million insect species, with about 1 million insect species currently found and described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stork2018_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stork2018-47">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">E. O. Wilson</a> has estimated that the number of insects living at any one time are around 10 quintillion (10 billion billion).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 950,000 and 1,000,000 of all described species are insects, so over 50% of all described eukaryotes (1.8 million) are insects (see illustration). With only 950,000 known non-insects, if the actual number of insects is 5.5 million, they may represent over 80% of the total. As only about 20,000 new species of all organisms are described each year, most insect species may remain undescribed, unless the rate of species descriptions greatly increases. Of the 24 orders of insects, four dominate in terms of numbers of described species; at least 670,000 identified species belong to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coleoptera" title="Coleoptera">Coleoptera</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diptera" title="Diptera">Diptera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hymenoptera" title="Hymenoptera">Hymenoptera</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Percent_of_insect_species.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="203" data-file-width="262" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Percent_of_insect_species.svg/220px-Percent_of_insect_species.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Percent_of_insect_species.svg/330px-Percent_of_insect_species.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Percent_of_insect_species.svg/440px-Percent_of_insect_species.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Percent_of_insect_species.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Insects with population trends documented by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a>, for orders <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collembola" title="Collembola">Collembola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hymenoptera" title="Hymenoptera">Hymenoptera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odonata" title="Odonata">Odonata</a>,</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Orthoptera" title="Orthoptera">Orthoptera</a>.</i> Of 203 insect species that had such documented population trends in 2013, 33% were in decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dirzo2014_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dirzo2014-49">[48]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>As of 2017, at least 66 insect species extinctions had been recorded in the previous 500 years, generally on oceanic islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briggs2017_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs2017-50">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Decline_in_insect_populations" title="Decline in insect populations">Declines in insect abundance</a> have been attributed to artificial lighting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owens2018_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owens2018-51">[50]</a></sup> land use changes such as urbanization or agricultural use,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tscharntke2005_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tscharntke2005-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> pesticide use,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> and invasive species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> Studies summarized in a 2019 review suggested that a large proportion of insect species is threatened with extinction in the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wyckhuys2019_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wyckhuys2019-56">[55]</a></sup> The ecologist Manu Sanders notes that the 2019 review was biased by mostly excluding data showing increases or stability in insect population, with the studies limited to specific geographic areas and specific groups of species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> A larger 2020 meta-study, analyzing data from 166 long-term surveys, suggested that populations of terrestrial insects are decreasing rapidly, by about 9% per decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanKlink2020_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanKlink2020-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> Claims of pending mass insect extinctions or "insect apocalypse" based on a subset of these studies have been popularized in news reports, but often extrapolate beyond the study data or <a href="/wiki/Science_by_press_conference" title="Science by press conference">hyperbolize study findings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA2019_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA2019-60">[59]</a></sup> Other areas have shown increases in some insect species, although trends in most regions are currently unknown. It is difficult to assess long-term trends in insect abundance or diversity because historical measurements are generally not known for many species. Robust data to assess at-risk areas or species is especially lacking for arctic and tropical regions and a majority of the southern hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA2019_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-ESA2019-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology_and_physiology">Morphology and physiology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Insect_morphology" title="Insect morphology">Insect morphology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insect_physiology" title="Insect physiology">Insect physiology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="External">External</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Insect_anatomy_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1380" data-file-width="1970" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Insect_anatomy_diagram.svg/300px-Insect_anatomy_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Insect_anatomy_diagram.svg/450px-Insect_anatomy_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Insect_anatomy_diagram.svg/600px-Insect_anatomy_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Insect_anatomy_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Insect morphology<br/> <b>A</b>- Head <b>B</b>- Thorax <b>C</b>- Abdomen <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1022511316">.mw-parser-output .thumb .image-key{column-count:2}.mw-parser-output .thumb .image-key-wide{column-count:3}.mw-parser-output .thumb .image-key-narrow{column-count:1}.mw-parser-output .thumb .image-key>ol{margin-left:1.3em;margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .thumb .image-key>ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .thumb .image-key li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="image-key"><ol><li><a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antenna</a></li><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocellus" title="Ocellus">ocellus</a> (lower)</li><li>ocellus (upper)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Compound_eye" title="Compound eye">compound eye</a></li><li>brain (cerebral <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Prothorax" title="Prothorax">prothorax</a></li><li>dorsal blood vessel</li><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesothorax" title="Mesothorax">mesothorax</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Metathorax" title="Metathorax">metathorax</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Insect_wing" title="Insect href="/wiki/Malpighian_tubule_system" title="Malpighian tubule system">Malpighian tubules</a></li><li>tarsal pads</li><li>claws</li><li><a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg" title="Arthropod leg">tarsus</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg" title="Arthropod leg">tibia</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg" title="Arthropod leg">femur</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg" title="Arthropod leg">trochanter</a></li><li>foregut (crop, gizzard)</li><li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ventral_nerve_chord&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ventral nerve chord (page does not exist)">thoracic ganglion</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg" title="Arthropod leg">coxa</a></li><li>salivary gland</li><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subesophageal_ganglion" title="Subesophageal ganglion">subesophageal ganglion</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Insect_mouthparts" title="Insect mouthparts">mouthparts</a></li></ol></div></div></div></div>
<p>Insects have <a href="/wiki/Segmentation_(biology)" title="Segmentation (biology)">segmented</a> bodies supported by <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeletons</a>, the hard outer covering made mostly of <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>. The segments of the body are organized into three distinctive but interconnected units, or <a href="/wiki/Tagma_(biology)" title="Tagma (biology)">tagmata</a>: a head, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorax_(insect_anatomy)" title="Thorax (insect anatomy)">thorax</a> and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdomen_(insect_anatomy)" title="Abdomen (insect anatomy)">abdomen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> The head supports a pair of sensory <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a>, a pair of <a href="/wiki/Compound_eye" title="Compound eye">compound eyes</a>, zero to three simple eyes (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocelli" title="Ocelli">ocelli</a>) and three sets of variously modified appendages that form the <a href="/wiki/Insect_mouthparts" title="Insect mouthparts">mouthparts</a>. The thorax is made up of three segments: the prothorax, mesothorax and the metathorax. Each thoracic segment supports one pair of legs. The meso- and metathoracic segments may each have a pair of <a href="/wiki/Insect_wing" title="Insect wing">wings</a>, depending on the insect. The abdomen consists of eleven segments, though in a few species of insects, these segments may be fused together or reduced in size. The abdomen also contains most of the <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">respiratory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Excretion" title="Excretion">excretory</a> and reproductive internal structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 22–48">: 22–48 </span></sup> Considerable variation and many adaptations in the body parts of insects occur, especially wings, legs, antenna and mouthparts.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Segmentation">Segmentation</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Head" title="Head">head</a> is enclosed in a hard, heavily sclerotized, unsegmented, exoskeletal head capsule, or <a href="/wiki/Epicranium" title="Epicranium">epicranium</a>, which contains most of the sensing organs, including the antennae, ocellus or eyes, and the mouthparts. Of all the insect orders, Orthoptera displays the most features found in other insects, including the <a href="/wiki/Suture_(anatomy)" title="Suture (anatomy)">sutures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sclerite" title="Sclerite">sclerites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReshCarde2009_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReshCarde2009-62">[61]</a></sup> Here, the <a href="/wiki/Vertex_(anatomy)" title="Vertex (anatomy)">vertex</a>, or the apex (dorsal region), is situated between the compound eyes for insects with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypognathous" title="Hypognathous">hypognathous</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opisthognathous" title="Opisthognathous">opisthognathous</a> head. In prognathous insects, the vertex is not found between the compound eyes, but rather, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocellus" title="Ocellus">ocelli</a> are normally. This is because the primary axis of the head is rotated 90° to become parallel to the primary axis of the body. In some species, this region is modified and assumes a different name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReshCarde2009_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReshCarde2009-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 13">: 13 </span></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Thorax" title="Thorax">thorax</a> is a tagma composed of three sections, the <a href="/wiki/Prothorax" title="Prothorax">prothorax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesothorax" title="Mesothorax">mesothorax</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Metathorax" title="Metathorax">metathorax</a>. The anterior segment, closest to the head, is the prothorax, with the major features being the first pair of legs and the pronotum. The middle segment is the mesothorax, with the major features being the second pair of legs and the anterior wings. The third and most posterior segment, abutting the abdomen, is the metathorax, which features the third pair of legs and the posterior wings. Each segment is delineated by an intersegmental suture. Each segment has four basic regions. The dorsal surface is called the tergum (or <i>notum</i>) to distinguish it from the abdominal terga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup> The two lateral regions are called the pleura (singular: pleuron) and the ventral aspect is called the sternum. In turn, the notum of the prothorax is called the pronotum, the notum for the mesothorax is called the mesonotum and the notum for the metathorax is called the metanotum. Continuing with this logic, the mesopleura and metapleura, as well as the mesosternum and metasternum, are used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReshCarde2009_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReshCarde2009-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a> is the largest tagma of the insect, which typically consists of 11–12 segments and is less strongly sclerotized than the head or thorax. Each segment of the abdomen is represented by a sclerotized tergum and sternum. Terga are separated from each other and from the adjacent sterna or pleura by membranes. Spiracles are located in the pleural area. Variation of this ground plan includes the fusion of terga or terga and sterna to form continuous dorsal or ventral shields or a conical tube. Some insects bear a sclerite in the pleural area called a laterotergite. Ventral sclerites are sometimes called laterosternites. During the embryonic stage of many insects and the postembryonic stage of primitive insects, 11 abdominal segments are present. In modern insects there is a tendency toward reduction in the number of the abdominal segments, but the primitive number of 11 is maintained during embryogenesis. Variation in abdominal segment number is considerable. If the Apterygota are considered to be indicative of the ground plan for pterygotes, confusion reigns: adult Protura have 12 segments, Collembola have 6. The orthopteran family Acrididae has 11 segments, and a fossil specimen of Zoraptera has a 10-segmented abdomen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReshCarde2009_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReshCarde2009-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Exoskeleton">Exoskeleton</span></h4>
<p>The insect outer skeleton, the cuticle, is made up of two layers: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicuticle" title="Epicuticle">epicuticle</a>, which is a thin and waxy water resistant outer layer and contains no <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>, and a lower layer called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Procuticle" title="Procuticle">procuticle</a>. The procuticle is chitinous and much thicker than the epicuticle and has two layers: an outer layer known as the exocuticle and an inner layer known as the endocuticle. The tough and flexible endocuticle is built from numerous layers of fibrous chitin and proteins, criss-crossing each other in a sandwich pattern, while the exocuticle is rigid and <a href="/wiki/Sclerotization" title="Sclerotization">hardened</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 22–24">: 22–24 </span></sup> The exocuticle is greatly reduced in many insects during their <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larval</a> stages, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar" title="Caterpillar">caterpillars</a>. It is also reduced in soft-bodied adult insects.
</p><p>Insects are the only <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> to have developed active flight capability, and this has played an important role in their success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 186">: 186 </span></sup> Their flight muscles are able to contract multiple times for each single nerve impulse, allowing the wings to beat faster than would ordinarily be possible.
</p><p>Having their muscles attached to their exoskeletons is efficient and allows more muscle connections.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal">Internal</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nervous_system">Nervous system</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> of an insect can be divided into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insect_brain" title="Insect brain">brain</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Ventral_nerve_cord" title="Ventral nerve cord">ventral nerve cord</a>. The head capsule is made up of six fused segments, each with either a pair of <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a>, or a cluster of nerve cells outside of the brain. The first three pairs of ganglia are fused into the brain, while the three following pairs are fused into a structure of three pairs of ganglia under the insect's <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>, called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subesophageal_ganglion" title="Subesophageal ganglion">subesophageal ganglion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-5"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 57">: 57 </span></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thoracic" title="Thoracic">thoracic</a> segments have one ganglion on each side, which are connected into a pair, one pair per segment. This arrangement is also seen in the abdomen but only in the first eight segments. Many species of insects have reduced numbers of ganglia due to fusion or reduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INSECTS:_ROLE_OF_THE_SPIRACLES_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-INSECTS:_ROLE_OF_THE_SPIRACLES-63">[62]</a></sup> Some cockroaches have just six ganglia in the abdomen, whereas the wasp <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vespa_crabro" title="Vespa crabro">Vespa crabro</a></i> has only two in the thorax and three in the abdomen. Some insects, like the house fly <i><a href="/wiki/Housefly" title="Housefly">Musca domestica</a></i>, have all the body ganglia fused into a single large thoracic ganglion. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>At least some insects have <a href="/wiki/Nociceptor" title="Nociceptor">nociceptors</a>, cells that detect and transmit signals responsible for the sensation of <a href="/wiki/Pain_in_animals" title="Pain in animals">pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (May 2017)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> This was discovered in 2003 by studying the variation in reactions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Larvae" title="Larvae">larvae</a> of the common fruit-fly <a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">Drosophila</a> to the touch of a heated probe and an unheated one. The larvae reacted to the touch of the heated probe with a stereotypical rolling behavior that was not exhibited when the larvae were touched by the unheated probe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Nociception" title="Nociception">nociception</a> has been demonstrated in insects, there is no consensus that insects feel pain consciously<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Insects are capable of learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Digestive_system">Digestive system</span></h4>
<p>An insect uses its digestive system to extract nutrients and other substances from the food it consumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-genent_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-genent-70">[69]</a></sup> Most of this food is ingested in the form of <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecules</a> and other complex substances like <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>. These macromolecules must be broken down by <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolic reactions</a> into smaller molecules like <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_sugar" title="Simple sugar">simple sugars</a> before being used by cells of the body for energy, growth, or reproduction. This break-down process is known as <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a>.
</p><p>There is extensive variation among different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomic_order" title="Taxonomic order">orders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">life stages</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Caste" title="Caste">castes</a> in the digestive system of insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> This is the result of extreme adaptations to various lifestyles. The present description focuses on a generalized composition of the digestive system of an adult orthopteroid insect, which is considered basal to interpreting particularities of other groups.
</p><p>The main structure of an insect's digestive system is a long enclosed tube called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alimentary_canal" title="Alimentary canal">alimentary canal</a>, which runs lengthwise through the body. The alimentary canal directs food unidirectionally from the <a href="/wiki/Mouth" title="Mouth">mouth</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a>. It has three sections, each of which performs a different process of digestion. In addition to the alimentary canal, insects also have paired salivary glands and salivary reservoirs. These structures usually reside in the thorax, adjacent to the foregut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-6"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 70–77">: 70–77 </span></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Salivary_gland" title="Salivary gland">salivary glands</a> (element 30 in numbered diagram) in an insect's mouth produce saliva. The salivary ducts lead from the glands to the reservoirs and then forward through the head to an opening called the salivarium, located behind the hypopharynx. By moving its mouthparts (element 32 in numbered diagram) the insect can mix its food with saliva. The mixture of saliva and food then travels through the salivary tubes into the mouth, where it begins to break down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duncan1939_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duncan1939-73">[72]</a></sup> Some insects, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diptera" title="Diptera">flies</a>, have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mastication" title="Mastication">extra-oral digestion</a>. Insects using extra-oral digestion expel digestive enzymes onto their food to break it down. This strategy allows insects to extract a significant proportion of the available nutrients from the food source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Insect_Physiology_and_Biochemistry_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Insect_Physiology_and_Biochemistry-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 31">: 31 </span></sup> The gut is where almost all of insects' digestion takes place. It can be divided into the <a href="/wiki/Foregut" title="Foregut">foregut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">midgut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindgut" title="Hindgut">hindgut</a>.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Foregut">Foregut</span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malpighian_tube.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="921" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Malpighian_tube.svg/250px-Malpighian_tube.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Malpighian_tube.svg/375px-Malpighian_tube.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Malpighian_tube.svg/500px-Malpighian_tube.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malpighian_tube.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stylized diagram of insect digestive tract showing <a href="/wiki/Malpighian_tubule_system" title="Malpighian tubule system">malpighian tubule</a>, from an insect of the order <a href="/wiki/Orthoptera" title="Orthoptera">Orthoptera</a></div></div></div>
<p>The first section of the alimentary canal is the <a href="/wiki/Foregut" title="Foregut">foregut</a> (element 27 in numbered diagram), or stomodaeum. The foregut is lined with a cuticular lining made of <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> as protection from tough food. The foregut includes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buccal_cavity" title="Buccal cavity">buccal cavity</a> (mouth), <a href="/wiki/Pharynx" title="Pharynx">pharynx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crop_(anatomy)" title="Crop (anatomy)">crop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proventriculus" title="Proventriculus">proventriculus</a> (any part may be highly modified), which both store food and signify when to continue passing onward to the midgut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-7"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 70">: 70 </span></sup>
</p><p>Digestion starts in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buccal_cavity" title="Buccal cavity">buccal cavity</a> (mouth) as partially chewed food is broken down by saliva from the salivary glands. As the salivary glands produce fluid and <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">carbohydrate-digesting enzymes</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Amylase" title="Amylase">amylases</a>), strong muscles in the pharynx pump fluid into the buccal cavity, lubricating the food like the salivarium does, and helping blood feeders, and xylem and phloem feeders.
</p><p>From there, the pharynx passes food to the esophagus, which could be just a simple tube passing it on to the crop and proventriculus, and then onward to the midgut, as in most insects. Alternately, the foregut may expand into a very enlarged crop and proventriculus, or the crop could just be a <a href="/wiki/Diverticulum" title="Diverticulum">diverticulum</a>, or fluid-filled structure, as in some Diptera species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Insect_Physiology_and_Biochemistry_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Insect_Physiology_and_Biochemistry-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 30–31">: 30–31 </span></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Midgut">Midgut</span></h5>
<p>Once food leaves the crop, it passes to the <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">midgut</a> (element 13 in numbered diagram), also known as the mesenteron, where the majority of digestion takes place. Microscopic projections from the midgut wall, called <a href="/wiki/Microvillus" title="Microvillus">microvilli</a>, increase the surface area of the wall and allow more nutrients to be absorbed; they tend to be close to the origin of the midgut. In some insects, the role of the microvilli and where they are located may vary. For example, specialized microvilli producing digestive enzymes may more likely be near the end of the midgut, and absorption near the origin or beginning of the midgut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Insect_Physiology_and_Biochemistry_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Insect_Physiology_and_Biochemistry-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 32">: 32 </span></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Hindgut">Hindgut</span></h5>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Hindgut" title="Hindgut">hindgut</a> (element 16 in numbered diagram), or proctodaeum, undigested food particles are joined by <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a> to form fecal pellets. The rectum absorbs 90% of the water in these fecal pellets, and the dry pellet is then eliminated through the anus (element 17), completing the process of digestion. Envaginations at the anterior end of the hindgut form the Malpighian tubules, which form the main excretory system of insects.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Excretory_system">Excretory system</span></h4>
<p>Insects may have one to hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Malpighian_tubule_system" title="Malpighian tubule system">Malpighian tubules</a> (element 20). These tubules remove nitrogenous wastes from the hemolymph of the insect and regulate osmotic balance. Wastes and solutes are emptied directly into the alimentary canal, at the junction between the midgut and hindgut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-8"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 71–72, 78–80">: 71–72, 78–80 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_system">Reproductive system</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Insect_reproductive_system" title="Insect reproductive system">Insect reproductive system</a></div>
<p>The reproductive system of female insects consist of a pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovaries" title="Ovaries">ovaries</a>, accessory glands, one or more <a href="/wiki/Spermatheca" title="Spermatheca">spermathecae</a>, and ducts connecting these parts. The ovaries are made up of a number of egg tubes, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovarioles" title="Ovarioles">ovarioles</a>, which vary in size and number by species. The number of eggs that the insect is able to make vary by the number of ovarioles with the rate that eggs can develop being also influenced by ovariole design. Female insects are able make eggs, receive and store sperm, manipulate sperm from different males, and lay eggs. Accessory glands or glandular parts of the oviducts produce a variety of substances for sperm maintenance, transport and fertilization, as well as for protection of eggs. They can produce glue and protective substances for coating eggs or tough coverings for a batch of eggs called <a href="/wiki/Ootheca" title="Ootheca">oothecae</a>. Spermathecae are tubes or sacs in which sperm can be stored between the time of mating and the time an egg is fertilized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReshCarde2009_62-4"><a href="#cite_note-ReshCarde2009-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 880">: 880 </span></sup>
</p><p>For males, the reproductive system is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Testis" title="Testis">testis</a>, suspended in the body cavity by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invertebrate_trachea" title="Invertebrate trachea">tracheae</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Fat_body" title="Fat body">fat body</a>. Most male insects have a pair of testes, inside of which are sperm tubes or follicles that are enclosed within a membranous sac. The follicles connect to the vas deferens by the vas efferens, and the two tubular vasa deferentia connect to a median ejaculatory duct that leads to the outside. A portion of the vas deferens is often enlarged to form the seminal vesicle, which stores the sperm before they are discharged into the female. The seminal vesicles have glandular linings that secrete nutrients for nourishment and maintenance of the sperm. The ejaculatory duct is derived from an invagination of the epidermal cells during development and, as a result, has a cuticular lining. The terminal portion of the ejaculatory duct may be sclerotized to form the intromittent organ, the aedeagus. The remainder of the male reproductive system is derived from embryonic <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesoderm</a>, except for the germ cells, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spermatogonia" title="Spermatogonia">spermatogonia</a>, which descend from the primordial pole cells very early during embryogenesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReshCarde2009_62-5"><a href="#cite_note-ReshCarde2009-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 885">: 885 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiratory_system">Respiratory system</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="656" data-file-width="656" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png/220px-Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png/330px-Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png/440px-Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Structural_organization_of_the_heart_of_the_mosquito_Anopheles_gambiae_-_image.ppat.v08.i11.g001.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The tube-like heart (green) of the mosquito <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles_gambiae" title="Anopheles gambiae">Anopheles gambiae</a></i> extends horizontally across the body, interlinked with the diamond-shaped <a href="/wiki/Insect_wing#Muscles" title="Insect wing">wing muscles</a> (also green) and surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Pericardium" title="Pericardium">pericardial cells</a> (red). Blue depicts <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_nuclei" title="Cell nuclei">cell nuclei</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system_of_insects" title="Respiratory system of insects">Insect respiration</a> is accomplished without <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>. Instead, the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system_of_insects" title="Respiratory system of insects">insect respiratory system</a> uses a system of internal tubes and sacs through which gases either diffuse or are actively pumped, delivering oxygen directly to tissues that need it via their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invertebrate_trachea" title="Invertebrate trachea">trachea</a> (element 8 in numbered diagram). In most insects, air is taken in through openings on the sides of the abdomen and thorax called <a href="/wiki/Spiracle_(arthropods)" title="Spiracle (arthropods)">spiracles</a>.
</p><p>The respiratory system is an important factor that limits the size of insects. As insects get larger, this type of oxygen transport is less efficient and thus the heaviest insect currently weighs less than 100 g. However, with increased atmospheric oxygen levels, as were present in the late <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a>, larger insects were possible, such as dragonflies with wingspans of more than two feet (60 cm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are many different patterns of <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a> demonstrated by different groups of insects. Gas exchange patterns in insects can range from continuous and <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffusive</a> ventilation, to <a href="/wiki/Discontinuous_gas_exchange" title="Discontinuous gas exchange">discontinuous gas exchange</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-9"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 65–68">: 65–68 </span></sup> During continuous gas exchange, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> is taken in and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> is released in a continuous cycle. In discontinuous gas exchange, however, the insect takes in oxygen while it is active and small amounts of carbon dioxide are released when the insect is at rest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPE_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPE-76">[75]</a></sup> Diffusive ventilation is simply a form of continuous gas exchange that occurs by <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffusion</a> rather than physically taking in the oxygen. Some species of insect that are submerged also have adaptations to aid in respiration. As larvae, many insects have gills that can extract oxygen dissolved in water, while others need to rise to the water surface to replenish air supplies, which may be held or trapped in special structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aquins_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-aquins-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulatory_system">Circulatory system</span></h4>
<p>Because oxygen is delivered directly to tissues via tracheoles, the circulatory system is not used to carry oxygen, and is therefore greatly reduced. The insect circulatory system is open; it has no <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a> or <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arteries</a>, and instead consists of little more than a single, perforated dorsal tube that pulses <a href="/wiki/Peristalsis" title="Peristalsis">peristaltically</a>. This dorsal blood vessel (element 14) is divided into two sections: the heart and aorta. The dorsal blood vessel circulates the <a href="/wiki/Hemolymph" title="Hemolymph">hemolymph</a>, arthropods' fluid analog of <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>, from the rear of the body cavity forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-10"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 61–65">: 61–65 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> Hemolymph is composed of plasma in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemocytes" title="Hemocytes">hemocytes</a> are suspended. Nutrients, hormones, wastes, and other substances are transported throughout the insect body in the hemolymph. Hemocytes include many types of cells that are important for immune responses, wound healing, and other functions. Hemolymph pressure may be increased by muscle contractions or by swallowing air into the digestive system to aid in molting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> Hemolymph is also a major part of the open <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthropods" title="Arthropods">arthropods</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiders" title="Spiders">spiders</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustaceans" title="Crustaceans">crustaceans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_development">Reproduction and development</span></h2>
