href="/wiki/Simosyrphus_grandicornis" title="Simosyrphus grandicornis">Simosyrphus grandicornis</a></i> hoverflies mating in flight.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MatingGrasshopper.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2133" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/MatingGrasshopper.jpg/200px-MatingGrasshopper.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/MatingGrasshopper.jpg/300px-MatingGrasshopper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/MatingGrasshopper.jpg/400px-MatingGrasshopper.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grasshoppers" title="Grasshoppers">grasshoppers</a> mating.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The majority of insects hatch from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a>. The fertilization and development takes place inside the egg, enclosed by a shell (<a href="/wiki/Chorion" title="Chorion">chorion</a>) that consists of maternal tissue. In contrast to eggs of other arthropods, most insect eggs are drought resistant. This is because inside the chorion two additional membranes develop from embryonic tissue, the <a href="/wiki/Amnion" title="Amnion">amnion</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serosa" title="Serosa">serosa</a>. This serosa secretes a <a href="/wiki/Cuticle" title="Cuticle">cuticle</a> rich in <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> that protects the embryo against desiccation. In <a href="/wiki/Schizophora" title="Schizophora">Schizophora</a> however the serosa does not develop, but these flies lay their eggs in damp places, such as rotting matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> Some species of insects, like the cockroach <i><a href="/wiki/Blaptica_dubia" title="Blaptica dubia">Blaptica dubia</a></i>, as well as juvenile aphids and tsetse flies, are <a href="/wiki/Ovoviviparity" title="Ovoviviparity">ovoviviparous</a>. The eggs of ovoviviparous animals develop entirely inside the female, and then hatch immediately upon being laid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgraw-hill_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-mcgraw-hill-6">[6]</a></sup> Some other species, such as those in the genus of cockroaches known as <i><a href="/wiki/Diploptera" title="Diploptera">Diploptera</a></i>, are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viviparous" title="Viviparous">viviparous</a>, and thus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gestate" title="Gestate">gestate</a> inside the mother and are <a href="/wiki/Viviparity" title="Viviparity">born alive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-11"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 129, 131, 134–135">: 129, 131, 134–135 </span></sup> Some insects, like parasitic wasps, show <a href="/wiki/Polyembryony" title="Polyembryony">polyembryony</a>, where a single fertilized egg divides into many and in some cases thousands of separate embryos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-12"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 136–137">: 136–137 </span></sup> Insects may be <i>univoltine</i>, <i>bivoltine</i> or <i>multivoltine</i>, i.e. they may have one, two or many broods (generations) in a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OdonateGlossary_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-OdonateGlossary-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orgyia_recens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1188" data-file-width="927" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Orgyia_recens.jpg/220px-Orgyia_recens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Orgyia_recens.jpg/330px-Orgyia_recens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Orgyia_recens.jpg/440px-Orgyia_recens.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orgyia_recens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The different forms of the male (top) and female (bottom) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tussock_moth" title="Tussock moth">tussock moth</a> <i>Orgyia recens</i> is an example of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a> in insects.</div></div></div>
<p>Other developmental and reproductive variations include <a href="/wiki/Haplodiploidy" title="Haplodiploidy">haplodiploidy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">polymorphism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paedomorphosis" title="Paedomorphosis">paedomorphosis</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peramorphosis" title="Peramorphosis">peramorphosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthenogenesis" title="Parthenogenesis">parthenogenesis</a> and more rarely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermaphroditism" title="Hermaphroditism">hermaphroditism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-13"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 143">: 143 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> In haplodiploidy, which is a type of <a href="/wiki/Sex-determination_system" title="Sex-determination system">sex-determination system</a>, the offspring's sex is determined by the number of sets of <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a> an individual receives. This system is typical in bees and wasps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> Polymorphism is where a species may have different <i>morphs</i> or <i>forms</i>, as in the <a href="/wiki/Amblycorypha_oblongifolia" title="Amblycorypha oblongifolia">oblong winged katydid</a>, which has four different varieties: green, pink and yellow or tan. Some insects may retain <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypes</a> that are normally only seen in juveniles; this is called paedomorphosis. In peramorphosis, an opposite sort of phenomenon, insects take on previously unseen traits after they have matured into adults. Many insects display sexual dimorphism, in which males and females have notably different appearances, such as the moth <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orgyia_recens" title="Orgyia recens">Orgyia recens</a></i> as an exemplar of sexual dimorphism in insects.
</p><p>Some insects use <a href="/wiki/Parthenogenesis" title="Parthenogenesis">parthenogenesis</a>, a process in which the female can reproduce and give birth without having the eggs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization" title="Fertilization">fertilized</a> by a <a href="/wiki/Male" title="Male">male</a>. Many aphids undergo a form of parthenogenesis, called cyclical parthenogenesis, in which they alternate between one or many generations of asexual and sexual reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> In summer, aphids are generally female and parthenogenetic; in the autumn, males may be produced for sexual reproduction. Other insects produced by parthenogenesis are bees, wasps and ants, in which they spawn males. However, overall, most individuals are female, which are produced by fertilization. The males are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a> and the females are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgraw-hill_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-mcgraw-hill-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Insect life-histories show adaptations to withstand cold and dry conditions. Some temperate region insects are capable of activity during winter, while some others migrate to a warmer climate or go into a state of <a href="/wiki/Torpor" title="Torpor">torpor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Still other insects have evolved mechanisms of <a href="/wiki/Diapause" title="Diapause">diapause</a> that allow eggs or pupae to survive these conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metamorphosis">Metamorphosis</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">Metamorphosis</a> in insects is the biological process of development all insects must undergo. There are two forms of metamorphosis: incomplete metamorphosis and complete metamorphosis.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Incomplete_metamorphosis">Incomplete metamorphosis</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hemimetabolism" title="Hemimetabolism">Hemimetabolism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hemimetabolism" title="Hemimetabolism">Hemimetabolous</a> insects, those with incomplete metamorphosis, change gradually by undergoing a series of <a href="/wiki/Ecdysis" title="Ecdysis">molts</a>. An insect molts when it outgrows its exoskeleton, which does not stretch and would otherwise restrict the insect's growth. The molting process begins as the insect's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidermis_(skin)" title="Epidermis (skin)">epidermis</a> secretes a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicuticle" title="Epicuticle">epicuticle</a> inside the old one. After this new epicuticle is secreted, the epidermis releases a mixture of enzymes that digests the endocuticle and thus detaches the old cuticle. When this stage is complete, the insect makes its body swell by taking in a large quantity of water or air, which makes the old cuticle split along predefined weaknesses where the old exocuticle was thinnest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-14"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 142">: 142 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P523To524_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppertFoxBarnes2004P523To524-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Immature insects that go through incomplete metamorphosis are called <a href="/wiki/Nymph_(biology)" title="Nymph (biology)">nymphs</a> or in the case of dragonflies and damselflies, also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naiad_(insect)" title="Naiad (insect)">naiads</a>. Nymphs are similar in form to the adult except for the presence of wings, which are not developed until adulthood. With each molt, nymphs grow larger and become more similar in appearance to adult insects.
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:2008px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aeshna_cyanea_freshly_slipped_L2.jpg" title="This southern hawker dragonfly molts its exoskeleton several times during its life as a nymph; shown is the final molt to become a winged adult (eclosion)."><img alt="" data-file-height="1240" data-file-width="12065" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Aeshna_cyanea_freshly_slipped_L2.jpg/2000px-Aeshna_cyanea_freshly_slipped_L2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Aeshna_cyanea_freshly_slipped_L2.jpg/3000px-Aeshna_cyanea_freshly_slipped_L2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Aeshna_cyanea_freshly_slipped_L2.jpg/4000px-Aeshna_cyanea_freshly_slipped_L2.jpg 2x" width="2000"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aeshna_cyanea_freshly_slipped_L2.jpg" title="File:Aeshna cyanea freshly slipped L2.jpg"> </a></div>This <a href="/wiki/Southern_hawker" title="Southern hawker">southern hawker</a> dragonfly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molt" title="Molt">molts</a> its exoskeleton several times during its life as a <a href="/wiki/Nymph_(biology)" title="Nymph (biology)">nymph</a>; shown is the final molt to become a winged adult (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eclosion" title="Eclosion">eclosion</a>).</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Complete_metamorphosis">Complete metamorphosis</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Holometabolism" title="Holometabolism">Holometabolism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gulf_Fritillary_Life_Cycle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="424" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Gulf_Fritillary_Life_Cycle.svg/250px-Gulf_Fritillary_Life_Cycle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Gulf_Fritillary_Life_Cycle.svg/375px-Gulf_Fritillary_Life_Cycle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Gulf_Fritillary_Life_Cycle.svg/500px-Gulf_Fritillary_Life_Cycle.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gulf_Fritillary_Life_Cycle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gulf_fritillary" title="Gulf fritillary">Gulf fritillary</a> life cycle, an example of <a href="/wiki/Holometabolism" title="Holometabolism">holometabolism</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Holometabolism" title="Holometabolism">Holometabolism</a>, or complete metamorphosis, is where the insect changes in four stages, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">egg</a> or <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larva</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Adult" title="Adult">adult</a> or <a href="/wiki/Imago" title="Imago">imago</a>. In these species, an egg hatches to produce a <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larva</a>, which is generally worm-like in form. This worm-like form can be one of several varieties: eruciform (caterpillar-like), scarabaeiform (grub-like), campodeiform (elongated, flattened and active), elateriform (wireworm-like) or vermiform (maggot-like). The larva grows and eventually becomes a <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupa</a>, a stage marked by reduced movement and often sealed within a <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">cocoon</a>. There are three types of pupae: obtect, exarate or coarctate. Obtect pupae are compact, with the legs and other appendages enclosed. Exarate pupae have their legs and other appendages free and extended. Coarctate pupae develop inside the larval skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-15"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 151">: 151 </span></sup> Insects undergo considerable change in form during the pupal stage, and emerge as adults. Butterflies are a well-known example of insects that undergo complete metamorphosis, although most insects use this life cycle. Some insects have evolved this system to <a href="/wiki/Hypermetamorphosis" title="Hypermetamorphosis">hypermetamorphosis</a>.
</p><p>Complete metamorphosis is a trait of the most diverse insect group, the <a href="/wiki/Endopterygota" title="Endopterygota">Endopterygota</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-16"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 143">: 143 </span></sup> Endopterygota includes 11 Orders, the largest being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diptera" title="Diptera">Diptera</a> (flies), <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a> (butterflies and moths), and <a href="/wiki/Hymenoptera" title="Hymenoptera">Hymenoptera</a> (bees, wasps, and ants), and <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">Coleoptera</a> (beetles). This form of development is exclusive to insects and not seen in any other arthropods.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Senses_and_communication">Senses and communication</span></h2>
<p>Many insects possess very sensitive and specialized organs of <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">perception</a>. Some insects such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bees" title="Bees">bees</a> can perceive <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> wavelengths, or detect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polarized_light" title="Polarized light">polarized light</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a> of male moths can detect the <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a> of female moths over distances of many kilometers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> The yellow paper wasp (<i><a href="/wiki/Polistes_versicolor" title="Polistes versicolor">Polistes versicolor</a></i>) is known for its wagging movements as a form of communication within the colony; it can waggle with a frequency of 10.6±2.1 Hz (n=190). These wagging movements can signal the arrival of new material into the nest and aggression between workers can be used to stimulate others to increase foraging expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> There is a pronounced tendency for there to be a trade-off between visual acuity and chemical or tactile acuity, such that most insects with well-developed eyes have reduced or simple antennae, and vice versa. There are a variety of different mechanisms by which insects perceive sound; while the patterns are not universal, insects can generally hear sound if they can produce it. Different insect species can have varying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_hearing" title="Animal hearing">hearing</a>, though most insects can hear only a narrow range of frequencies related to the frequency of the sounds they can produce. Mosquitoes have been found to hear up to 2 kHz, and some grasshoppers can hear up to 50 kHz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> Certain predatory and parasitic insects can detect the characteristic sounds made by their prey or hosts, respectively. For instance, some nocturnal moths can perceive the <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasonic</a> emissions of <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a>, which helps them avoid predation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-17"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 87–94">: 87–94 </span></sup> Insects that feed on blood have special sensory structures that can detect <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> emissions, and use them to home in on their hosts.
</p><p>Some insects display a rudimentary <a href="/wiki/Numeracy" title="Numeracy">sense of numbers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> such as the solitary wasps that prey upon a single species. The mother wasp lays her eggs in individual cells and provides each egg with a number of live caterpillars on which the young feed when hatched. Some species of wasp always provide five, others twelve, and others as high as twenty-four caterpillars per cell. The number of caterpillars is different among species, but always the same for each sex of larva. The male solitary wasp in the genus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eumenes_(genus)" title="Eumenes (genus)">Eumenes</a></i> is smaller than the female, so the mother of one species supplies him with only five caterpillars; the larger female receives ten caterpillars in her cell.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Light_production_and_vision">Light production and vision</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calliphora_sp_Portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1928" data-file-width="1294" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Calliphora_sp_Portrait.jpg/170px-Calliphora_sp_Portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Calliphora_sp_Portrait.jpg/255px-Calliphora_sp_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Calliphora_sp_Portrait.jpg/340px-Calliphora_sp_Portrait.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calliphora_sp_Portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Most insects have compound eyes and two antennae.</div></div></div>
<p>A few insects, such as members of the families Poduridae and Onychiuridae (Collembola), <a href="/wiki/Mycetophilidae" title="Mycetophilidae">Mycetophilidae</a> (Diptera) and the beetle families <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lampyridae" title="Lampyridae">Lampyridae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phengodidae" title="Phengodidae">Phengodidae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elateridae" title="Elateridae">Elateridae</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Staphylinidae" title="Staphylinidae">Staphylinidae</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioluminescent" title="Bioluminescent">bioluminescent</a>. The most familiar group are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fireflies" title="Fireflies">fireflies</a>, beetles of the family Lampyridae. Some species are able to control this light generation to produce flashes. The function varies with some species using them to attract mates, while others use them to lure prey. Cave dwelling larvae of <i><a href="/wiki/Arachnocampa" title="Arachnocampa">Arachnocampa</a></i> (Mycetophilidae, fungus gnats) glow to lure small flying insects into sticky strands of silk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> Some fireflies of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Photuris" title="Photuris">Photuris</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Mimicry" title="Mimicry">mimic</a> the flashing of female <i><a href="/wiki/Photinus_(beetle)" title="Photinus (beetle)">Photinus</a></i> species to attract males of that species, which are then captured and devoured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> The colors of emitted light vary from dull blue (<i>Orfelia fultoni</i>, Mycetophilidae) to the familiar greens and the rare reds (<i>Phrixothrix tiemanni</i>, Phengodidae).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most insects, except some species of <a href="/wiki/Rhaphidophoridae" title="Rhaphidophoridae">cave crickets</a>, are able to perceive light and dark. Many species have acute vision capable of detecting minute movements. The eyes may include simple eyes or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocellus" title="Ocellus">ocelli</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Compound_eye" title="Compound eye">compound eyes</a> of varying sizes. Many species are able to detect light in the <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a> wavelengths. Color vision has been demonstrated in many species and phylogenetic analysis suggests that UV-green-blue <a href="/wiki/Trichromacy" title="Trichromacy">trichromacy</a> existed from at least the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period between 416 and 359 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-insvision_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-insvision-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The individual lenses in compound eyes are immobile, and it was therefore presumed that insects were not able to focus. But research on fruit flies, which is the only insects studied so far, has shown that photoreceptor cells underneath each lens move rapidly in and out of focus in a series of movements called photoreceptor microsaccades. This gives them a much clearer image of the world than previously assumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sound_production_and_hearing">Sound production and hearing</span></h3>
<p>Insects were the earliest organisms to produce and sense sounds. Hearing has evolved independently at least 19 times in different insect groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> Insects make sounds mostly by mechanical action of appendages. In <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a> and crickets, this is achieved by <a href="/wiki/Stridulation" title="Stridulation">stridulation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cicada" title="Cicada">Cicadas</a> make the loudest sounds among the insects by producing and amplifying sounds with special modifications to their body to form <a href="/wiki/Tymbal" title="Tymbal">tymbals</a> and associated musculature. The African <a href="/wiki/Cicada" title="Cicada">cicada</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Brevisana_brevis" title="Brevisana brevis">Brevisana brevis</a></i> has been measured at 106.7 <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">decibels</a> at a distance of 50 cm (20 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-walker_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-walker-102">[101]</a></sup> Some insects, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Helicoverpa_zea" title="Helicoverpa zea">Helicoverpa zea</a></i> moths, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawk_moth" title="Hawk moth">hawk moths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hedylidae" title="Hedylidae">Hedylid</a> butterflies, can hear <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a> and take evasive action when they sense that they have been detected by bats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> Some moths produce ultrasonic clicks that were once thought to have a role in jamming bat <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocation</a>. The ultrasonic clicks were subsequently found to be produced mostly by unpalatable moths to warn bats, just as <a href="/wiki/Aposematism" title="Aposematism">warning colorations</a> are used against predators that hunt by sight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> Some otherwise palatable moths have evolved to <a href="/wiki/Batesian_mimicry" title="Batesian mimicry">mimic</a> these calls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> More recently, the claim that some moths can jam bat sonar has been revisited. Ultrasonic recording and high-speed infrared videography of bat-moth interactions suggest the palatable tiger moth really does defend against attacking big brown bats using ultrasonic clicks that jam bat sonar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096940132">.mw-parser-output .listen .side-box-text{line-height:1.1em}.mw-parser-output .listen-plain{border:none;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded{width:100%;margin:0;border-width:1px 0 0 0;background:transparent}.mw-parser-output .listen-header{padding:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen-embedded .listen-header{padding:2px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen-file-header{padding:4px 0}.mw-parser-output .listen .description{padding-top:2px}.mw-parser-output .listen .mw-tmh-player{max-width:100%}@media(max-width:719px){.mw-parser-output .listen{clear:both}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .listen:not(.listen-noimage){width:320px}.mw-parser-output .listen-left{overflow:visible;float:left}.mw-parser-output .listen-center{float:none;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-left listen noprint listen-left">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Grasshoppers.ogg" title="File:Grasshoppers.ogg">Grasshopper stridulation</a></div>
<div><audio controls="" data-durationhint="18" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Grasshoppers.ogg" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:232px;" width="232"><source data-bandwidth="121077" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (121 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/Grasshoppers.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="314984" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/28/Grasshoppers.ogg/Grasshoppers.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div>
<div class="description">Several unidentified grasshoppers stridulating</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>Very low sounds are also produced in various species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coleoptera" title="Coleoptera">Coleoptera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hymenoptera" title="Hymenoptera">Hymenoptera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mantodea" title="Mantodea">Mantodea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuroptera" title="Neuroptera">Neuroptera</a>. These low sounds are simply the sounds made by the insect's movement. Through microscopic stridulatory structures located on the insect's muscles and joints, the normal sounds of the insect moving are amplified and can be used to warn or communicate with other insects. Most sound-making insects also have <a href="/wiki/Tympanal_organ" title="Tympanal organ">tympanal organs</a> that can perceive airborne sounds. Some species in <a href="/wiki/Hemiptera" title="Hemiptera">Hemiptera</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Corixidae" title="Corixidae">corixids</a> (water boatmen), are known to communicate via underwater sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> Most insects are also able to sense <a href="/wiki/Vibration" title="Vibration">vibrations</a> transmitted through surfaces.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><audio class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="11" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Field_cricket_Gryllus_pennsylvanicus.ogg" id="mwe_player_1" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="73484" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (73 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/80/Field_cricket_Gryllus_pennsylvanicus.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="185392" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/80/Field_cricket_Gryllus_pennsylvanicus.ogg/Field_cricket_Gryllus_pennsylvanicus.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><track data-dir="ltr" kind="subtitles" label="English ‪(en)‬" src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3AField_cricket_Gryllus_pennsylvanicus.ogg&amp;lang=en&amp;trackformat=vtt&amp;origin=%2A" srclang="en" type="text/vtt"/></audio> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Field_cricket_Gryllus_pennsylvanicus.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cricket in garage with familiar call.</div></div></div>
<p>Communication using surface-borne vibrational signals is more widespread among insects because of size constraints in producing air-borne sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> Insects cannot effectively produce low-frequency sounds, and high-frequency sounds tend to disperse more in a dense environment (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foliage" title="Foliage">foliage</a>), so insects living in such environments communicate primarily using substrate-borne vibrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> The mechanisms of production of vibrational signals are just as diverse as those for producing sound in insects.
</p><p>Some species use vibrations for communicating within members of the same species, such as to attract mates as in the songs of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shield_bug" title="Shield bug">shield bug</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Nezara_viridula" title="Nezara viridula">Nezara viridula</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> Vibrations can also be used to communicate between entirely different species; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycaenid" title="Lycaenid">lycaenid</a> (gossamer-winged butterfly) caterpillars, which are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myrmecophile" title="Myrmecophile">myrmecophilous</a> (living in a mutualistic association with ants) communicate with ants in this way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Madagascar_hissing_cockroach" title="Madagascar hissing cockroach">Madagascar hissing cockroach</a> has the ability to press air through its spiracles to make a hissing noise as a sign of aggression;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Death%27s-head_hawkmoth" title="Death's-head hawkmoth">death's-head hawkmoth</a> makes a squeaking noise by forcing air out of their pharynx when agitated, which may also reduce aggressive worker honey bee behavior when the two are in close proximity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_communication">Chemical communication</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chemical_communication_in_insects" title="Chemical communication in insects">Chemical communication in insects</a></div>
<p>Chemical communications in animals rely on a variety of aspects including taste and smell. Chemoreception is the physiological response of a sense organ (i.e. taste or smell) to a chemical stimulus where the chemicals act as signals to regulate the state or activity of a cell. A semiochemical is a message-carrying chemical that is meant to attract, repel, and convey information. Types of semiochemicals include pheromones and kairomones. One example is the butterfly <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phengaris_arion" title="Phengaris arion">Phengaris arion</a> </i>which uses chemical signals as a form of mimicry to aid in predation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corruption_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corruption-115">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the use of sound for communication, a wide range of insects have evolved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_communication" title="Chemical communication">chemical means for communication</a>. These <a href="/wiki/Semiochemical" title="Semiochemical">semiochemicals</a> are often derived from plant metabolites including those meant to attract, repel and provide other kinds of information. <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">Pheromones</a>, a type of semiochemical, are used for attracting mates of the opposite sex, for aggregating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conspecificity" title="Conspecificity">conspecific</a> individuals of both sexes, for deterring other individuals from approaching, to mark a trail, and to trigger aggression in nearby individuals. <a href="/wiki/Allomone" title="Allomone">Allomones</a> benefit their producer by the effect they have upon the receiver. <a href="/wiki/Kairomone" title="Kairomone">Kairomones</a> benefit their receiver instead of their producer. Synomones benefit the producer and the receiver. While some chemicals are targeted at individuals of the same species, others are used for communication across species. The use of scents is especially well-developed in social insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-18"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 96–105">: 96–105 </span></sup> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023754711">.mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#b1d2ff}</style><span class="vanchor"><span id="Cuticular_hydrocarbons"></span><span id="Cuticular_hydrocarbon"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Cuticular hydrocarbons</span></span> are nonstructural materials produced and secreted to the cuticle surface to fight <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>. They are important, too, as pheromones, especially in social insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yan-Liebig-2021_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yan-Liebig-2021-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_behavior">Social behavior</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Termite_Cathedral_DSC03570.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2272" data-file-width="1704" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Termite_Cathedral_DSC03570.jpg/220px-Termite_Cathedral_DSC03570.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Termite_Cathedral_DSC03570.jpg/330px-Termite_Cathedral_DSC03570.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Termite_Cathedral_DSC03570.jpg/440px-Termite_Cathedral_DSC03570.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Termite_Cathedral_DSC03570.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cathedral mound created by termites (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoptera" title="Isoptera">Isoptera</a>).</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_insect" title="Social insect">Social insects</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a> and many <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a>, are the most familiar species of <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">eusocial</a> animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> They live together in large well-organized colonies that may be so tightly integrated and genetically similar that the colonies of some species are sometimes considered <a href="/wiki/Superorganism" title="Superorganism">superorganisms</a>. It is sometimes argued that the various species of <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bee</a> are the only invertebrates (and indeed one of the few non-human groups) to have evolved a system of abstract symbolic communication where a behavior is used to represent and convey specific information about something in the environment. In this communication system, called <a href="/wiki/Bee_learning_and_communication" title="Bee learning and communication">dance language</a>, the angle at which a bee dances represents a direction relative to the sun, and the length of the dance represents the distance to be flown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-19"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 309–311">: 309–311 </span></sup> Though perhaps not as advanced as honey bees, <a href="/wiki/Bumblebee" title="Bumblebee">bumblebees</a> also potentially have some social communication behaviors. <i><a href="/wiki/Bombus_terrestris" title="Bombus terrestris">Bombus terrestris</a></i>, for example, exhibit a faster learning curve for visiting unfamiliar, yet rewarding flowers, when they can see a conspecific foraging on the same species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Only insects that live in nests or colonies demonstrate any true capacity for fine-scale spatial orientation or homing. This can allow an insect to return unerringly to a single hole a few millimeters in diameter among thousands of apparently identical holes clustered together, after a trip of up to several kilometers' distance. In a phenomenon known as <a href="/wiki/Philopatry" title="Philopatry">philopatry</a>, insects that <a href="/wiki/Hibernation" title="Hibernation">hibernate</a> have shown the ability to recall a specific location up to a year after last viewing the area of interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> A few insects seasonally <a href="/wiki/Insect_migration" title="Insect migration">migrate</a> large distances between different geographic regions (e.g., the overwintering areas of the <a href="/wiki/Monarch_butterfly" title="Monarch butterfly">monarch butterfly</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-20"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 14">: 14 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Care_of_young">Care of young</span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eusocial" title="Eusocial">eusocial</a> insects build nests, guard eggs, and provide food for offspring full-time.
Most insects, however, lead short lives as adults, and rarely interact with one another except to mate or compete for mates. A small number exhibit some form of <a href="/wiki/Parental_care" title="Parental care">parental care</a>, where they will at least guard their eggs, and sometimes continue guarding their offspring until adulthood, and possibly even feeding them. Another simple form of parental care is to construct a nest (a burrow or an actual construction, either of which may be simple or complex), store provisions in it, and lay an egg upon those provisions. The adult does not contact the growing offspring, but it nonetheless does provide food. This sort of care is typical for most species of bees and various types of wasps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Locomotion">Locomotion</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight">Flight</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Insect_flight" title="Insect flight">Insect flight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insect_wing" title="Insect wing">Insect wing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Motion_of_Insectwing.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Motion_of_Insectwing.gif/250px-Motion_of_Insectwing.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Motion_of_Insectwing.gif/375px-Motion_of_Insectwing.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Motion_of_Insectwing.gif 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Motion_of_Insectwing.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Basic motion of the insect wing in insect with an indirect flight mechanism scheme of dorsoventral cut through a thorax segment with <b>a</b> wings, <b>b</b> joints, <b>c</b> dorsoventral muscles, <b>d</b> longitudinal muscles.</div></div></div>
<p>Insects are the only group of <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> to have developed flight. The evolution of insect wings has been a subject of debate. Some <a href="/wiki/Entomology" title="Entomology">entomologists</a> suggest that the wings are from paranotal lobes, or extensions from the insect's exoskeleton called the <a href="/wiki/Notum" title="Notum">nota</a>, called the <i>paranotal theory</i>. Other theories are based on a <a href="/wiki/Pleuron_(insect_anatomy)" title="Pleuron (insect anatomy)">pleural</a> origin. These theories include suggestions that wings originated from modified gills, spiracular flaps or as from an appendage of the epicoxa. The <i>epicoxal theory</i> suggests the insect wings are modified epicoxal exites, a modified appendage at the base of the legs or <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg" title="Arthropod leg">coxa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> age, some of the <i><a href="/wiki/Meganeura" title="Meganeura">Meganeura</a></i> dragonflies had as much as a 50 cm (20 in) wide wingspan. The appearance of gigantic insects has been found to be consistent with high atmospheric oxygen. The respiratory system of insects constrains their size, however the high oxygen in the atmosphere allowed larger sizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> The largest flying insects today are much smaller, with the largest wingspan belonging to the white witch moth (<i><a href="/wiki/Thysania_agrippina" title="Thysania agrippina">Thysania agrippina</a></i>), at approximately 28 cm (11 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIR_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIR-123">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Insect flight has been a topic of great interest in <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamics" title="Aerodynamics">aerodynamics</a> due partly to the inability of steady-state theories to explain the lift generated by the tiny wings of insects. But insect wings are in motion, with flapping and vibrations, resulting in churning and <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">eddies</a>, and the misconception that physics says <a href="/wiki/Bumblebee#Misconception_about_flight" title="Bumblebee">"bumblebees can't fly"</a> persisted throughout most of the twentieth century.
</p><p>Unlike <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, many small insects are swept along by the <a href="/wiki/Prevailing_winds" title="Prevailing winds">prevailing winds</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> although many of the larger insects are known to make <a href="/wiki/Insect_migration" title="Insect migration">migrations</a>. <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">Aphids</a> are known to be transported long distances by low-level <a href="/wiki/Jet_stream" title="Jet stream">jet streams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> As such, fine line patterns associated with converging winds within <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a> imagery, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WSR-88D" title="WSR-88D">WSR-88D</a> radar network, often represent large groups of insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> Radar can also be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insect_radar" title="Insect radar">deliberately used to monitor insects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Long-et-al-2020_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Long-et-al-2020-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Walking">Walking</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="45" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Alternating_Tripod_Gait.webm" height="188" id="mwe_player_2" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Alternating_Tripod_Gait.webm/250px--Alternating_Tripod_Gait.webm.jpg" preload="none" width="250"><source data-bandwidth="146904" data-framerate="10" data-height="480" data-shorttitle="VP9 480P" data-title="SD VP9 (480P)" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/61/Alternating_Tripod_Gait.webm/Alternating_Tripod_Gait.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="306328" data-framerate="10" data-height="720" data-shorttitle="VP9 720P" data-title="HD VP9 (720P)" data-transcodekey="720p.vp9.webm" data-width="960" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/61/Alternating_Tripod_Gait.webm/Alternating_Tripod_Gait.webm.720p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm;
