codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alternating_Tripod_Gait.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spatial and temporal stepping pattern of walking desert ants performing an alternating tripod gait. Recording rate: 500 fps, Playback rate: 10 fps.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_locomotion" title="Terrestrial locomotion">Terrestrial locomotion</a></div>
<p>Many adult insects use six legs for walking, with an alternating tripod <a href="/wiki/Gait" title="Gait">gait</a>. This allows for rapid walking while always having a stable stance; it has been studied extensively in <a href="/wiki/Cockroach" title="Cockroach">cockroaches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>. For the first step, the middle right leg and the front and rear left legs are in contact with the ground and move the insect forward, while the front and rear right leg and the middle left leg are lifted and moved forward to a new position. When they touch the ground to form a new stable triangle the other legs can be lifted and brought forward in turn and so on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Locomotion_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Locomotion-128">[127]</a></sup> The purest form of the tripedal gait is seen in insects moving at high speeds. However, this type of locomotion is not rigid and insects can adapt a variety of gaits. For example, when moving slowly, turning, avoiding obstacles, climbing or slippery surfaces, four (tetrapod) or more feet (wave-gait<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup>) may be touching the ground. Insects can also adapt their gait to cope with the loss of one or more limbs.
</p><p>Cockroaches are among the fastest insect runners and, at full speed, adopt a bipedal run to reach a high velocity in proportion to their body size. As cockroaches move very quickly, they need to be video recorded at several hundred frames per second to reveal their gait. More sedate locomotion is seen in the stick insects or walking sticks (<a href="/wiki/Phasmatodea" title="Phasmatodea">Phasmatodea</a>). A few insects have evolved to walk on the surface of the water, especially members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_strider" title="Water strider">Gerridae</a> family, commonly known as water striders. A few species of ocean-skaters in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Halobates" title="Halobates">Halobates</a></i> even live on the surface of open oceans, a habitat that has few insect species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Insect walking is of particular interest as practical form of <a href="/wiki/Robot_locomotion" title="Robot locomotion">robot locomotion</a>. The study of insects and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biped" title="Biped">bipeds</a> has a significant impact on possible robotic methods of transport. This may allow new <a href="/wiki/Hexapod_(robotics)" title="Hexapod (robotics)">hexapod robots</a> to be designed that can traverse <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">terrain</a> that robots with <a href="/wiki/Wheel" title="Wheel">wheels</a> may be unable to handle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Locomotion_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Locomotion-128">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Swimming">Swimming</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquatic_insects" title="Aquatic insects">Aquatic insects</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Notonecta_glauca01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="585" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Notonecta_glauca01.jpg/220px-Notonecta_glauca01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Notonecta_glauca01.jpg/330px-Notonecta_glauca01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Notonecta_glauca01.jpg/440px-Notonecta_glauca01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Notonecta_glauca01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The backswimmer <i><a href="/wiki/Notonecta_glauca" title="Notonecta glauca">Notonecta glauca</a></i> underwater, showing its paddle-like hindleg adaptation</div></div></div>
<p>A large number of insects live either part or the whole of their lives underwater. In many of the more primitive orders of insect, the immature stages are spent in an aquatic environment. Some groups of insects, like certain <a href="/wiki/Water_beetle" title="Water beetle">water beetles</a>, have aquatic adults as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aquins_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-aquins-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of these species have adaptations to help in under-water locomotion. Water beetles and water bugs have legs adapted into paddle-like structures. Dragonfly <a href="/wiki/Nymph_(biology)" title="Nymph (biology)">naiads</a> use jet propulsion, forcibly expelling water out of their rectal chamber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> Some species like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_strider" title="Water strider">water striders</a> are capable of walking on the surface of water. They can do this because their claws are not at the tips of the legs as in most insects, but recessed in a special groove further up the leg; this prevents the claws from piercing the water's surface film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aquins_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-aquins-77">[76]</a></sup> Other insects such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Staphylinidae" title="Staphylinidae">Rove beetle</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Stenus" title="Stenus">Stenus</a></i> are known to emit pygidial gland secretions that reduce surface tension making it possible for them to move on the surface of water by <a href="/wiki/Marangoni_effect" title="Marangoni effect">Marangoni propulsion</a> (also known by the <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> term <i>Entspannungsschwimmen</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Insect_ecology" title="Insect ecology">Insect ecology</a></div>
<p>Insect ecology is the scientific study of how insects, individually or as a community, interact with the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environment</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InsectEcology_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-InsectEcology-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3">: 3 </span></sup> Insects play one of the most important roles in their ecosystems, which includes many roles, such as soil turning and aeration, dung burial, pest control, pollination and wildlife nutrition. An example is the <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">scavengers</a> that feed on dead animals and fallen trees and thereby <a href="/wiki/Recycling" title="Recycling">recycle</a> biological materials into forms found useful by other <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> These insects, and others, are responsible for much of the process by which <a href="/wiki/Topsoil" title="Topsoil">topsoil</a> is created.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-21"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3, 218–228">: 3, 218–228 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defense_and_predation">Defense and predation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Defense_in_insects" title="Defense in insects">Defense in insects</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BatesMimButter.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="406" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/BatesMimButter.JPG/220px-BatesMimButter.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/BatesMimButter.JPG 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BatesMimButter.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Perhaps one of the most well-known examples of mimicry, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viceroy_butterfly" title="Viceroy butterfly">viceroy butterfly</a> (top) appears very similar to the <a href="/wiki/Monarch_butterfly" title="Monarch butterfly">monarch butterfly</a> (bottom).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritland_&amp;_Brower_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ritland_&amp;_Brower-136">[135]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Insects are mostly soft bodied, fragile and almost defenseless compared to other, larger lifeforms. The immature stages are small, move slowly or are immobile, and so all stages are exposed to <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasitism</a>. Insects then have a variety of defense strategies to avoid being attacked by predators or <a href="/wiki/Parasitoid" title="Parasitoid">parasitoids</a>. These include <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">camouflage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mimicry" title="Mimicry">mimicry</a>, toxicity and active defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans126_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Evans126-137">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">Camouflage</a> is an important defense strategy, which involves the use of coloration or shape to blend into the surrounding environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> This sort of protective coloration is common and widespread among beetle families, especially those that feed on wood or vegetation, such as many of the <a href="/wiki/Leaf_beetle" title="Leaf beetle">leaf beetles</a> (family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chrysomelidae" title="Chrysomelidae">Chrysomelidae</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Weevil" title="Weevil">weevils</a>. In some of these species, sculpturing or various colored scales or hairs cause the beetle to resemble bird dung or other inedible objects. Many of those that live in sandy environments blend in with the coloration of the substrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans126_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Evans126-137">[136]</a></sup> Most phasmids are known for effectively replicating the forms of sticks and leaves, and the bodies of some species (such as <i>O. macklotti</i> and <i>Palophus centaurus</i>) are covered in mossy or <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichenous</a> outgrowths that supplement their disguise. Very rarely, a species may have the ability to change color as their surroundings shift (<i>Bostra scabrinota</i>). In a further behavioral adaptation to supplement <a href="/wiki/Crypsis" title="Crypsis">crypsis</a>, a number of species have been noted to perform a rocking motion where the body is swayed from side to side that is thought to reflect the movement of leaves or twigs swaying in the breeze. Another method by which stick insects avoid predation and resemble twigs is by feigning death (<a href="/wiki/Catalepsy" title="Catalepsy">catalepsy</a>), where the insect enters a motionless state that can be maintained for a long period. The nocturnal feeding habits of adults also aids Phasmatodea in remaining concealed from predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bedford_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-bedford-139">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another defense that often uses color or shape to deceive potential enemies is <a href="/wiki/Mimicry" title="Mimicry">mimicry</a>. A number of <a href="/wiki/Longhorn_beetle" title="Longhorn beetle">longhorn beetles</a> (family Cerambycidae) bear a striking resemblance to <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a>, which helps them avoid predation even though the beetles are in fact harmless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans126_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-Evans126-137">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Batesian_mimicry" title="Batesian mimicry">Batesian</a> and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BCllerian_mimicry" title="Müllerian mimicry">Müllerian</a> <a href="/wiki/Mimicry" title="Mimicry">mimicry</a> complexes are commonly found in Lepidoptera. Genetic polymorphism and natural selection give rise to otherwise edible species (the mimic) gaining a survival advantage by resembling inedible species (the model). Such a mimicry complex is referred to as <i>Batesian</i>. One of the most famous examples, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viceroy_butterfly" title="Viceroy butterfly">viceroy butterfly</a> was long believed to be a Batesian mimic of the inedible <a href="/wiki/Monarch_butterfly" title="Monarch butterfly">monarch</a>, was later disproven, as the viceroy is more toxic than the monarch, and this resemblance is now considered to be a case of Müllerian mimicry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritland_&amp;_Brower_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ritland_&amp;_Brower-136">[135]</a></sup> In Müllerian mimicry, inedible species, usually within a taxonomic order, find it advantageous to resemble each other so as to reduce the sampling rate by predators who need to learn about the insects' inedibility. Taxa from the toxic genus <i><a href="/wiki/Heliconius" title="Heliconius">Heliconius</a></i> form one of the most well known Müllerian complexes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chemical defense is another important defense found among species of Coleoptera and Lepidoptera, usually being advertised by bright colors, such as the <a href="/wiki/Monarch_butterfly" title="Monarch butterfly">monarch butterfly</a>. They obtain their toxicity by sequestering the chemicals from the plants they eat into their own tissues. Some Lepidoptera manufacture their own toxins. Predators that eat poisonous butterflies and moths may become sick and vomit violently, learning not to eat those types of species; this is actually the basis of Müllerian mimicry. A predator who has previously eaten a poisonous lepidopteran may avoid other species with similar markings in the future, thus saving many other species as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANC_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANC-141">[140]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Ground_beetle" title="Ground beetle">ground beetles</a> of the family Carabidae can spray chemicals from their abdomen with great accuracy, to repel predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans126_137-3"><a href="#cite_note-Evans126-137">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollination">Pollination</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">Pollination</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1169" data-file-width="1754" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg/220px-Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg/330px-Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg/440px-Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apis_mellifera" title="Apis mellifera">European honey bee</a> carrying pollen in a <a href="/wiki/Pollen_basket" title="Pollen basket">pollen basket</a> back to the hive</div></div></div>
<p>Pollination is the process by which <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> is transferred in the reproduction of plants, thereby enabling <a href="/wiki/Fertilisation" title="Fertilisation">fertilisation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a>. Most flowering plants require an animal to do the transportation. While other animals are included as pollinators, the majority of pollination is done by insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USFS_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-USFS-142">[141]</a></sup> Because insects usually receive benefit for the pollination in the form of energy rich nectar it is a grand example of <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualism</a>. The various flower traits (and combinations thereof) that differentially attract one type of pollinator or another are known as <a href="/wiki/Pollination_syndrome" title="Pollination syndrome">pollination syndromes</a>. These arose through complex plant-animal adaptations. Pollinators find flowers through bright colorations, including ultraviolet, and attractant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pheromones" title="Pheromones">pheromones</a>. The study of pollination by insects is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Anthecology" title="Anthecology">anthecology</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasitism">Parasitism</span></h3>
<p>Many insects are parasites of other insects such as the <a href="/wiki/Parasitoid" title="Parasitoid">parasitoid</a> wasps. These insects are known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entomophagous_parasites" title="Entomophagous parasites">entomophagous parasites</a>. They can be beneficial due to their devastation of pests that can destroy crops and other resources. Many insects have a parasitic relationship with humans such as the mosquito. These insects are known to spread diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a> and because of such, mosquitoes indirectly cause more deaths of humans than any other animal.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_humans">Relationship to humans</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_pests">As pests</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pest_insect" title="Pest insect">Pest insect</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aedes_aegypti_biting_human.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1895" data-file-width="2391" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Aedes_aegypti_biting_human.jpg/220px-Aedes_aegypti_biting_human.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Aedes_aegypti_biting_human.jpg/330px-Aedes_aegypti_biting_human.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Aedes_aegypti_biting_human.jpg/440px-Aedes_aegypti_biting_human.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aedes_aegypti_biting_human.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Aedes_aegypti" title="Aedes aegypti">Aedes aegypti</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasite</a>, is the vector of <a href="/wiki/Dengue_fever" title="Dengue fever">dengue fever</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a></div></div></div>
<p>Many insects are considered <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">pests</a> by humans. Insects commonly regarded as pests include those that are parasitic (<i>e.g.</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lice" title="Lice">lice</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bed_bug_(insect)" title="Bed bug (insect)">bed bugs</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_diseases_caused_by_insects" title="List of diseases caused by insects">transmit diseases</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquitoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">flies</a>), damage structures (<a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>), or destroy agricultural goods (<a href="/wiki/Locust" title="Locust">locusts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weevil" title="Weevil">weevils</a>). Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entomologist" title="Entomologist">entomologists</a> are involved in various forms of pest control, as in research for companies to produce <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a>, but increasingly rely on methods of <a href="/wiki/Biological_pest_control" title="Biological pest control">biological pest control</a>, or biocontrol. Biocontrol uses one organism to reduce the population density of another organism—the pest—and is considered a key element of <a href="/wiki/Integrated_pest_management" title="Integrated pest management">integrated pest management</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the large amount of effort focused at controlling insects, human attempts to kill pests with insecticides can backfire. If used carelessly, the poison can kill all kinds of organisms in the area, including insects' natural predators, such as birds, mice and other insectivores. The effects of <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a>'s use exemplifies how some insecticides can threaten wildlife beyond intended populations of pest insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_beneficial_roles">In beneficial roles</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economic_entomology#Beneficial_insects" title="Economic entomology">Economic entomology § Beneficial insects</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:European_honey_bee_extracts_nectar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1620" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/European_honey_bee_extracts_nectar.jpg/220px-European_honey_bee_extracts_nectar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/European_honey_bee_extracts_nectar.jpg/330px-European_honey_bee_extracts_nectar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/European_honey_bee_extracts_nectar.jpg/440px-European_honey_bee_extracts_nectar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:European_honey_bee_extracts_nectar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Because they help flowering plants to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-pollination" title="Cross-pollination">cross-pollinate</a>, some insects are critical to agriculture. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_honey_bee" title="European honey bee">European honey bee</a> is gathering nectar while pollen collects on its body.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Common_brown_robberfly_with_prey.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Common_brown_robberfly_with_prey.jpg/220px-Common_brown_robberfly_with_prey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Common_brown_robberfly_with_prey.jpg/330px-Common_brown_robberfly_with_prey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Common_brown_robberfly_with_prey.jpg/440px-Common_brown_robberfly_with_prey.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Common_brown_robberfly_with_prey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robberfly" title="Robberfly">robberfly</a> with its prey, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoverfly" title="Hoverfly">hoverfly</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insectivorous" title="Insectivorous">Insectivorous</a> relationships such as these help control insect populations.</div></div></div>
<p>Although pest insects attract the most attention, many insects are beneficial to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environment</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>. Some insects, like <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butterfly" title="Butterfly">butterflies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollinate</a> <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>. Pollination is a <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualistic</a> relationship between plants and insects. As insects gather <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a> from different plants of the same species, they also spread <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> from plants on which they have previously fed. This greatly increases plants' ability to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-pollinate" title="Cross-pollinate">cross-pollinate</a>, which maintains and possibly even improves their evolutionary <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a>. This ultimately affects humans since ensuring healthy crops is critical to <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>. As well as pollination ants help with seed distribution of plants. This helps to spread the plants, which increases plant diversity. This leads to an overall better environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup> A serious environmental problem is the <a href="/wiki/Pollinator_decline" title="Pollinator decline">decline of populations of pollinator</a> insects, and a number of species of insects are now cultured primarily for <a href="/wiki/Pollination_management" title="Pollination management">pollination management</a> in order to have sufficient pollinators in the field, <a href="/wiki/Orchard" title="Orchard">orchard</a> or <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouse</a> at <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">bloom</a> time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_1991_Yearbook_of_Agriculture_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_1991_Yearbook_of_Agriculture-148">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 240–243">: 240–243 </span></sup> Another solution, as shown in Delaware, has been to raise native plants to help support native pollinators like <i><a href="/wiki/Lasioglossum_vierecki" title="Lasioglossum vierecki">L. vierecki</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farming_for_native_bees_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farming_for_native_bees-149">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>The economic value of pollination by insects has been estimated to be about $34 billion in the US alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Products made by insects</b>. Insects also produce useful substances such as <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">wax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lacquer" title="Lacquer">lacquer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>. <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">Honey bees</a> have been cultured by humans for thousands of years for honey, although contracting for crop pollination is becoming more significant for <a href="/wiki/Beekeeper" title="Beekeeper">beekeepers</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silkworm" title="Silkworm">silkworm</a> has greatly affected human history, as <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">silk-driven trade</a> established relationships between China and the rest of the world.
</p><p><b>Pest control</b>. <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">Insectivorous</a> insects, or insects that feed on other insects, are beneficial to humans if they eat insects that could cause damage to agriculture and human structures. For example, <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a> feed on crops and cause problems for farmers, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ladybug" title="Ladybug">ladybugs</a> feed on aphids, and can be used as a means to significantly reduce pest aphid populations. While <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> are perhaps more visible predators of insects, insects themselves account for the vast majority of insect consumption. Ants also help control animal populations by consuming small vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> Without predators to keep them in check, insects can undergo almost unstoppable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_explosion" title="Population explosion">population explosions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-22"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 328–348">: 328–348 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-23"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 400">: 400 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Medical uses</b>. Insects are also used in medicine, for example fly larvae (<a href="/wiki/Maggot" title="Maggot">maggots</a>) were formerly used to <a href="/wiki/Maggot_therapy" title="Maggot therapy">treat wounds</a> to prevent or stop <a href="/wiki/Gangrene" title="Gangrene">gangrene</a>, as they would only consume dead flesh. This treatment is finding modern usage in some hospitals. Recently insects have also gained attention as potential sources of drugs and other medicinal substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup> Adult insects, such as crickets and insect larvae of various kinds, are also commonly used as fishing bait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_research">In research</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_(aka).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="546" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg/220px-Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg/330px-Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg/440px-Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_%28aka%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Drosophila_melanogaster_-_side_(aka).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The common fruit fly <i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila_melanogaster" title="Drosophila melanogaster">Drosophila melanogaster</a></i> is one of the most widely used organisms in biological research.</div></div></div>
<p>Insects play important roles in biological research. For example, because of its small size, short generation time and high <a href="/wiki/Fecundity" title="Fecundity">fecundity</a>, the common fruit fly <i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila_melanogaster" title="Drosophila melanogaster">Drosophila melanogaster</a></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organism</a> for studies in the <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>. <i>D. melanogaster</i> has been an essential part of studies into principles like <a href="/wiki/Genetic_linkage" title="Genetic linkage">genetic linkage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epistasis" title="Epistasis">interactions between genes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomal</a> genetics, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evo-devo" title="Evo-devo">development</a>, behavior and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>. Because genetic systems are well conserved among eukaryotes, understanding basic cellular processes like <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a> in fruit flies can help to understand those processes in other eukaryotes, including humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pierce_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pierce-156">[155]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> of <i>D. melanogaster</i> was <a href="/wiki/Genome_project" title="Genome project">sequenced</a> in 2000, reflecting the organism's important role in biological research. It was found that 70% of the fly genome is similar to the human genome, supporting the evolution theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adams_2000_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adams_2000-157">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_food">As food</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Insects_as_food" title="Insects as food">Insects as food</a> and <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">Entomophagy</a></div>
<p>In some cultures, insects form part of the normal diet. In Africa, for instance, locally abundant species of both <a href="/wiki/Locust" title="Locust">locusts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a> are a common traditional human food source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> Some, especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep-fried" title="Deep-fried">deep-fried</a> <a href="/wiki/Cicada" title="Cicada">cicadas</a>, are considered to be <a href="/wiki/Delicacy" title="Delicacy">delicacies</a>. Insects have a high protein content for their mass, and some authors suggest their potential as a major source of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> in human <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutrition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-24"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 10–13">: 10–13 </span></sup> In most first-world countries, however, <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">entomophagy</a> (the eating of insects), is <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> They are also recommended by <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">militaries</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Survival_skills" title="Survival skills">survival</a> food for troops in adversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> Since it is impossible to eliminate pest insects from the human food chain, insects are inadvertently present in many foods, especially grains. <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">Food safety</a> laws in many countries do not prohibit insect parts in food, but rather limit their quantity. According to <a href="/wiki/Cultural_materialism_(anthropology)" title="Cultural materialism (anthropology)">cultural materialist</a> anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Harris" title="Marvin Harris">Marvin Harris</a>, the eating of insects is taboo in cultures that have other protein sources such as fish or livestock.
</p><p>Because of the abundance of insects and a worldwide concern of food shortages, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> considers that the world may have to, in the future, regard the prospects of eating insects as a food staple. Insects are noted for their nutrients, having a high content of protein, minerals and fats and are eaten by one-third of the global population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-investvine_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-investvine-161">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_feed">As feed</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Insects_as_feed" title="Insects as feed">Insects as feed</a></div>
<p>Several insect species such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_soldier_fly" title="Black soldier fly">black soldier fly</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Housefly" title="Housefly">housefly</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Maggot_farming" title="Maggot farming">maggot</a> forms, as well as beetle larvae such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mealworms" title="Mealworms">mealworms</a> can be processed and used as feed for farmed animals such as chicken, fish and pigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_other_products">In other products</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biorefinery" title="Biorefinery">Biorefinery</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_soldier_fly" title="Black soldier fly">Black soldier fly</a> larvae can provide <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, fats for use in <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>.
</p><p>Also, insect cooking oil, insect butter and fatty alcohols can be made from such insects as the superworm (<i><a href="/wiki/Zophobas_morio" title="Zophobas morio">Zophobas morio</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_pets">As pets</span></h3>
<p>Many species of insects are sold and kept as <a href="/wiki/Pet" title="Pet">pets</a>. There are special hobbyist magazines such as "Bugs" (now discontinued).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insects_in_culture" title="Insects in culture">Insects in culture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Scarabaeidae" title="Scarabaeidae">Scarab beetles</a> held religious and cultural symbolism in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Egypt" title="Old Egypt">Old Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and some shamanistic Old World cultures. The ancient <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">Chinese</a> regarded <a href="/wiki/Cicada" title="Cicada">cicadas</a> as symbols of rebirth or immortality. In <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a> literature, the epic poem of <a href="/wiki/Gilgamesh" title="Gilgamesh">Gilgamesh</a> has allusions to <a href="/wiki/Odonata" title="Odonata">Odonata</a> that signify the impossibility of immortality. Among the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">Aborigines</a> of <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Arrernte_language" title="Arrernte language">Arrernte</a> language groups, honey ants and <a href="/wiki/Witchetty_grub" title="Witchetty grub">witchetty grubs</a> served as personal clan totems. In the case of the 'San' bush-men of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalahari" title="Kalahari">Kalahari</a>, it is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Praying_mantis" title="Praying mantis">praying mantis</a> that holds much cultural significance including creation and zen-like patience in waiting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_37-25"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9">: 9 </span></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museum_of_New_Zealand" title="Museum of New Zealand">Museum of New Zealand</a> notes that "in everyday conversation", <i>bug</i> "refers to land arthropods with at least six legs, such as insects, spiders, and centipedes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>  In a chapter on "Bugs That Are Not Insects", entomologist Gilbert Walbauer specifies centipedes, millipedes, arachnids (spiders, <a href="/wiki/Opiliones" title="Opiliones">daddy longlegs</a>, scorpions, <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chigger" title="Chigger">chiggers</a> and ticks) as well as the few terrestrial crustaceans (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sowbug" title="Sowbug">sowbugs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pillbug" title="Pillbug">pillbugs</a>), <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walbauer5_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walbauer5-13">[13]</a></sup> but argues that "including legless creatures such as worms, slugs, and snails among the bugs stretches the word too much".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walbauer1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walbauer1-14">[14]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
